        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '893e3c9b-b559-4b5e-92d5-12dddbe74c27'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d233908607" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Herein, P2-type layered manganese and ruthenium oxide is synthesized as an outstanding intercalation cathode material for high-energy density Na-ion batteries (NIBs). P2-type sodium deficient transition metal oxide structure, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes where x varied between 0.05 and 0.5 are fabricated. The partially substituted main phase where x = 0.4 exhibits the best electrochemical performance with a discharge capacity of ≈170&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The in situ X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy (XAS) and time-resolved X-ray Diffraction (TR-XRD) measurements are performed to elucidate the neighborhood of the local structure and lattice parameters during cycling. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) revealed the oxygen-rich structure when Ru is introduced. Density of States (DOS) calculations revealed the Fermi-Level bandgap increases when Ru is doped, which enhances the electronic conductivity of the cathode. Furthermore, magnetization calculations revealed the presence of stronger Ru─O bonds and the stabilizing effect of Ru-doping on MnO6 octahedra. The results of Time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectroscopy (TOF-SIMS) revealed that the Ru-doped sample has more sodium and oxygenated-based species on the surface, while the inner layers mainly contain Ru–O and Mn–O species. The full cell study demonstrated the outstanding capacity retention where the cell maintained 70% of its initial capacity at 1&nbsp;C-rate after 500 cycles.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406332-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406332-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The proliferation of electronic devices and, notably, electric vehicles (EVs) has catalyzed significant advancements in electrochemical energy storage systems in recent years. While various types of electrical energy storage systems exist, it is important to highlight the remarkable progress of Li-ion batteries (LIBs) in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0001_R_d233908592e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Carbon Energy.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The demand for Li-ion batteries has surged, leading to an increase in the consumption of lithium sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0002_R_d233908592e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Newly developed electrode materials are expected to possess several key properties, including high energy density, long cycle life, and affordability for commercial feasibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0003_R_d233908592e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that LIBs, first introduced by Sony in the 1990s, have become one of the most extensively used battery systems for portable electronics and various other large-scale applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0004_R_d233908592e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Res. Bull.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, there has been significant research into alternative battery types, such as lithium–sulfur (Li–S) and lithium-air (Li-air), to improve the energy density of the current state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0005_R_d233908592e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, this progress has led to increased costs, primarily due to the limited and unevenly distributed lithium reserves around the globe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0006_R_d233908592e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>NIBs have appeared as a promising replacement toLIBs because their basic electrochemical properties are similar to LIBs, and they utilize sodium, which is much more abundant and more evenly distributed in the earth than that of lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0007_R_d233908592e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Carbon Energy.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Because of these advantages, NIBs present an environmentally friendly and cost-effective battery option with promising potential to meet the demands of large-scale energy storage, making them a viable alternative to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0008_R_d233908592e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Green Energy and Resources.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, several battery companies have emerged with a focus on commercializing sodium-ion cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0009_R_d233908592e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Conservation and Recycling.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is predicted that sodium-ion battery cells have the potential to swap lithium-ion cells in the near future, given the abundance of sodium and the lower costs associated with it providing a sustainable future for energy storage systems. Therefore, there are significant efforts in R&amp;D activities for new electrode materials used in sodium-ion cells. There are different structural compositions for the Na-ion cathodes such as the layered transition metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0010_R_d233908592e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Acc. Chem. Res.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> phosphate-based materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0011_R_d233908592e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> pyrophosphate-based materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0012_R_d233908592e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> fluorophosphate materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0013_R_d233908592e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> fluorosilicate-based materials<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0014_R_d233908592e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Technol.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and sulfate-based materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0015_R_d233908592e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Rec.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Manganese-based layered transition metal oxides are among the best materials targeted for the cathode development in sodium-ion batteries. They are gaining attention due to their low cost, high recyclability, and low toxicity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0016_R_d233908592e738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Select.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is known that Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> materials can form 2D layered or 3D tunnel structures, depending on their chemical composition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0017_R_d233908592e748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Materials.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Most of the Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> structures are classified into P2 and O3 types according to the number of Na<sup>+</sup> and MO<sub>2</sub> (M = transition metal) layers in the structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0018_R_d233908592e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P2-type Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> offers a structural advantage compared to O3-type. In the P2-type, Na<sup>+</sup> ions reside within the layers, and the broader rectangular spaces between adjacent layers are elongated. In this structure, Na ions are spread out across the trigonal prismatic regions, resulting in a better dispersion of ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0019_R_d233908592e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The most popular P2-type Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> composition can be formulated as Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>TM<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (where x = 0–1) where the TMs can be Fe, Cu, Ni, Co, V, or Ti, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0020_R_d233908592e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochim. Acta.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0021_R_d233908592e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to improve battery performance, the combination of the transition elements such as Ni, Cu, Fe, and Mn are among the most preferred elements for the high-performance sodium-containing layered metal oxides. Ruthenium (Ru) has a wide range of valence states when compared with iron in the structure. Considering the electron configuration of Ru, it has only one electron in its outermost shell, which allows it to form coordination complexes with various oxidation states. It has been established that Ru serves as an exceptional catalyst in oxidation reactions, displaying various oxidation states ranging from −2 to +8.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0022_R_d233908592e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Arabian J. Chem.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ability of Ru to exist in numerous oxidation states during cycling renders more electrons to be transferred, thereby enhancing battery capacity.</p>
         
         <p>This study successfully fabricated novel P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials using a modified solid-state reaction technique, and their structural properties were unraveled. It is found that Ru-ions were successfully substituted by Mn-ions in the structure and the highest capacities were obtained for the samples where x = 0.3 and 0.4. In situ XRD and XAS analysis of the cells were conducted to unravel the reaction mechanism of the cells. The full-cell performance of the optimized cell was studied, suggesting that Ru-substituted P2-type cathode materials hold significant potential for Na-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202406332-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202406332-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406332-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202406332-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The XRD diffraction patterns of the Ru-substituted cathode samples are given in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1a</a>. It is seen that the diffraction patterns are well matched by P2-type P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc symmetry which is typical Na–Mn–O cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0023_R_d233908592e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Technol.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the Ru content increased up to x = 0.5, an impurity phase of Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> is observed which has C2/c symmetry. This structure has the following unit parameters; a = 5.4141&nbsp;Å, b = 9.3663&nbsp;Å, and c = 10.8481&nbsp;Å.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0024_R_d233908592e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–f</a> (Supporting Information) displays each XRD pattern for samples doped with different amounts of Ru along with the GSAS refinement fitting results. It is clear that the sample where x&nbsp;=&nbsp;0.5 exhibits Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> peaks which are shown with the arrow. Nonetheless, the other samples were free of the impurity phase suggesting that the degree of impurity was limited to only the sample where x = 0.5. After the Rietveld-refinement of the samples, the calculated lattice parameters are given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). When examining the a- and c-lattice parameters, it is clear that while the a-lattice parameters remained constant with increasing Ru-content, the c-lattice parameters increased sharply between x = 0.1 and x = 0.2, and then when the value x is further increased, the c-lattice parameter decreased as the Ru-content increased. In addition to this, the Na-layer distance showed a similar trend with the c-parameters of the cells. The right panel of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1a</a> displays the 002 plane, which is directly affected by the c-direction. According to Bragg's law, shifting toward a lower angle in 2θ degrees indicates the crystal lattice expansion. This expansion trend is clearly seen in the peak of the (002) plane in the right panel of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1a</a> from x = 0.05 to x = 0.2. Afterward, the plane position is shifting toward higher angles indicating the shrinkage of the crystal lattice from x = 0.2 until x = 0.5. Overall, the trend in shifting direction of the peak bodes well with the fitting results. To understand the behavior of Ru-ions in the structure, we evaluated the valence state of the Mn-ions in Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> structure. In this structure, Mn-ions have 3.33+ valence state which means there are different ionic radii of Mn species. These species are speculated to be 0.72&nbsp;pm low spin (LS) and 0.785&nbsp;pm high spin (HS) for Mn<sup>3+</sup> and 0.67 pm for Mn<sup>4+</sup> existing in the octahedral coordination.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0023_R_d233908592e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Technol.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Because of this, there is a possibility of having Jahn–Teller active Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions in the lattice which may cause voltage hysteresis during the sodiation and desodiation process of the battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0024_R_d233908592e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the Ru-ions could have three possible different ion radii such as 0.82, 0.76, and 0.705&nbsp;pm for the valence states of 3+, 4+, and 5+, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0025_R_d233908592e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Acta Cryst.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is understood that the XRD peaks are shifted toward the lower angle region when the ion of higher ionic radii substitutes the ion of lower ionic radii due to expansion in the crystal lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0026_R_d233908592e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Alloys Compd.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1a</a>, we observed that the 002 peak shifts to a lower angle up to x = 0.2 and then shifts to a higher angle until x = 0.5. It is anticipated that when ruthenium (Ru) is substituted in low concentrations, the structure has a tendency toward low oxidation states like Ru<sup>3+</sup> because of its larger ionic radii. However, as the amount of substitution increases, the doped sample is more likely to favor lower ionic radii states, such as Ru<sup>4+</sup> and Ru<sup>5+</sup>. According to the charge balance of the chemical formula of Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, there are Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions in the samples.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c57ca777-a8d1-44c3-943f-d3d85666eb45/smll202406332-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c57ca777-a8d1-44c3-943f-d3d85666eb45/smll202406332-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bfeff58e-b4c9-46fc-b632-e694d7355977/smll202406332-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c57ca777-a8d1-44c3-943f-d3d85666eb45/smll202406332-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern and right panel shows (002) planes, b) FTIR, c) Raman analysis of the Ru substituted samples of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> where x varies between 0.05 and 0.5. d) the Survey data of XPS, e) Na1s, f) Mn2p, g) Ru3d, and h) O1s spectrums for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Ru substituted powders.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>When substituting Ru ions with Mn ions, several ion formation possibilities arise in the system: i) the substitution of Ru<sup>3+</sup> by Mn<sup>3+</sup>, which is feasible for maintaining the charge balance of the system. ii) the substitution of Ru<sup>3+</sup> by Mn<sup>4+</sup>, which leads to the oxidation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup> in the structure. iii) the substitution of Ru<sup>4+</sup> by Mn<sup>3+</sup>, resulting in the reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup>. When considering higher substitutions where x is above 0.2, the (002) peak shifts to higher angles. This shift indicates the substitution of Ru ions with lower ionic radii, such as Ru<sup>4+</sup> and Ru<sup>5+</sup>, instead of Ru<sup>3+</sup>. When we assume that the Ru<sup>5+</sup> ions penetrate into the main structure, some of Mn<sup>3+</sup> or Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions should transform to Mn<sup>2+</sup> ions (0.81 for LS and 0.97 for HS) or Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the structure, respectively. In this case, the lattice volume affects the ionic radii of Ru<sup>5+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> and there should be competition between these ions. When high-valence state Ru ions are present in the structure, maintaining charge balance becomes challenging. As a result, the material tends to absorb more oxygen to achieve charge equilibrium. This increased oxygen absorption can influence various properties of the material, such as its electronic structure and reactivity. We also speculate that due to the high valence state of Ru for the sample where x = 0.5, the structure also forms Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> impurity, shown in the Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2f</a> (Supporting Information). We like to note that the valence state of each element will be elaborated in the XPS section.</p>
            
            <p>The FTIR analysis results of the samples are given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1b</a> and it is observed that the bands at 485&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned as a – bending M–O–M modes of MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra), 552&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned b – asymmetric stretching vibrations of MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra), 604&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned c – asymmetric stretching vibrations of MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra), 703&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned d) which are the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0027_R_d233908592e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Catal.">27</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0028_R_d233908592e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The FTIR bands at 881&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned e) and 1438&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (assigned f) are related to Ru–O vibration in the structure<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0029_R_d233908592e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Clust. Sci.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0030_R_d233908592e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the absorbance of these two bands was increased by increasing Ru-content in the samples. This trend agrees well with the XRD results where x = 0.5 shows impurity of Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>The Raman spectrum of the Ru substituted Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> powders was given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1c</a> and six different peaks were observed around 214 (R<sub>1</sub>) cm<sup>−1</sup>, 272 (R<sub>2</sub>) cm<sup>−1</sup>, 373 (R<sub>3</sub>) cm<sup>−1</sup> and 578 (R<sub>4</sub>) cm<sup>−1</sup>. Posonov et. al. investigated the Raman shift of Li<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> which is the isostructure of Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> and they claimed that R<sub>1</sub> and R<sub>2</sub> peaks are related to A<sub>g</sub> modes of the C2/m phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0031_R_d233908592e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is well known that the R<sub>3</sub> and R<sub>4</sub> Raman peaks are related to P2 type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0032_R_d233908592e1110" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The band around R<sub>3</sub> can be explained by the E<sub>2g</sub> modes in the structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0033_R_d233908592e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the peak at R<sub>4</sub> is due to symmetric stretching-mode A<sub>1g</sub> in the lattice, which is explained by the motions of the oxygen atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0034_R_d233908592e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Based on the XRD analysis, we can infer that the valence state of transition metals is influenced by the increasing amount of Ru in the materials. This observation suggests that the amount of Ru can affect the oxidation states of other transition metals. To elucidate the change in the valence state of the metal ions in the structure, we performed the XPS analysis of the elements in the samples, and the survey data was presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1d</a>. The peaks of C1s, Mn2p, Ru3d, O1s, and Na1s for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 were observed at ≈280.1, ≈642.1, ≈284.8, ≈530.3, and ≈1070.9&nbsp;eV, respectively. The Na1s XPS data for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1e</a> exhibit nearly identical graphs. This similarity indicates that the valence state and crystal field around Na<sup>+</sup> ions remain constant albeit with different amount of Ru substitution. This finding aligns with the previous studies on similar compositions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0035_R_d233908592e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Batteries.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that the binding energy difference is called chemical shift and found as 1071.32, 1070.62, and 1070.85&nbsp;eV for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively. The positions of peaks can shift because of the chemical properties of nearby atoms and changes in the local environment around the element.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0036_R_d233908592e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Phys.: Conf. Series.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is anticipated that doping the cathode with Ru would alter the local structure of the primary phase. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1f</a> shows the XPS data of the Mn2p for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 samples suggesting that there are two main peaks of Mn2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn2p<sub>1/2</sub> which are consisted of Mn<sup>3+</sup> 641.9 (x = 0.1), 641.9 (x = 0.3), and 642.0 (x = 0.5) eV for Mn2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 653.4 (x = 0.1), 653.3 (x = 0.3), and 653.4 (x = 0.5) eV for Mn2p<sub>1/2</sub>. In case of Mn<sup>4+</sup> species, the peaks are located at 643.5 (x = 0.1), 643.6 (x = 0.3) and 643.7 (x = 0.1) eV for Mn2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 654.9 (x = 0.1), 654.9 (x = 0.3) and 655 (x = 0.5) eV for Mn2p<sub>1/2</sub> ions. These results are also consistent well with the previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0037_R_d233908592e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When we calculated the peak area of each of the Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> peaks in the XPS data, the ratios of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions were calculated as 64.3%/35.7%, 64.5%/35.5%, and 65.3%/34.7% for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively. When doping with Ru, we expect the average valence state of Ru in the samples to either match the average valence state of Mn or lead to an increase in the number of oxygen ions in the structure. The energy differences between the 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> levels correspond to the binding energy separation between the spin-split components which depends on the valence state of the transition metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0038_R_d233908592e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the differences for Mn<sup>3+</sup> were calculated as 11.5, 11.4, and 11.8&nbsp;eV for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, the energy gap for Mn<sup>4+</sup> was determined as 11.4, 11.3, and 11.3 for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1g</a>, we should indicate that the XPS spectrum of Ru3d in samples partially overlaps with the C1s spectrum. The C1s peaks that appeared in the samples are the overlapping of Ru3d spectra positioned at 285 and 289&nbsp;eV are related to adventitious carbon in the structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0039_R_d233908592e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanomaterials.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The observed peaks at 281.8, 281.74, and 281.74&nbsp;eV for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 are explained by Ru3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Ru<sup>4+</sup> ions in the structure which is consistent with the existing studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0040_R_d233908592e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We should note that the XPS peak area corresponding to Ru<sup>4+</sup> ions increased with higher doping levels in the structure. The O1s XPS spectrum of the x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 Ru substituted samples were given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0001">1h</a> and it is seen that there are two types of oxygen in the structure. The O1s sub-peaks ≈529.6&nbsp;eV for lower energy region can be explained by Ru─O bond in the sample as described earlier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0039_R_d233908592e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanomaterials.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although we didn't observe the sub-peaks of oxygen O1s spectrum for P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> type cathode materials in our previous studies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0020_R_d233908592e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochim. Acta.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0021_R_d233908592e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the second sub-peak of the XPS O1s spectrum is due to O<sup>2-</sup> metal-oxygen bond in the sodium metal oxide compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0040_R_d233908592e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the peak area of these two peaks, the lower energy peak ratios were calculated as 22.9%, 31.7%, and 52.3% for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively. There are small peak shifts for low energy (529.6, 529.7, and 529.9&nbsp;eV for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively) and high energy peaks (531.1, 531.3, and 531.6&nbsp;eV for x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5, respectively) of XPS data. This could be explained by the excess oxygen which implies that there is an electrostatic force effect between Ru and the surrounding oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0039_R_d233908592e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanomaterials.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0041_R_d233908592e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Electrochim. Acta.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The BET absorption and desorption graphs are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) and the obtained shape of the adsorption/desorption curves can be predicted as type IV isotherm which is typical for meso-porous materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0042_R_d233908592e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Microporous Mesoporous Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The surface area of the samples was calculated using the data in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) and BET equations and obtained as 1.05, 2.81, 2.28, 1.78, 1.23, and 1.31&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for x = 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5, respectively. It can be concluded that the surface areas and pore structures of the Ru-doped materials exhibit similar surface properties. This suggests that the Ru dopant does not significantly alter the surface characteristics of the materials. The surface morphology of the Ru-substituted samples, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a–f</a> (Supporting Information), reveals similar grain formations characterized by arbitrarily shaped grains with varying geometries and sizes larger than 1&nbsp;µm. Additionally, the substitution of Ru in the main phase leads to an increase in the size of these grains. This is especially more pronounced in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4e,f</a> (Supporting Information). It is well known that the total free energy change is related to surface-free energy and volume-free energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0043_R_d233908592e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Metals.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As grains grow, the relative contribution of surface energy decreases. Consequently, the material with larger grains tends to have a more stable structure compared to a material with smaller grains. It is predicted that the substitution of Ru in the main phase results in an increase in the stability of the produced powders. This increased stability could be attributed to the changes in grain size and surface energy discussed earlier. In such a scenario, the crystallinity of the material is expected to improve, leading to more intense peaks in the XRD pattern, as indicated in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). This improvement in crystallinity is often associated with increased stability and enhanced material properties. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a–c</a> (Supporting Information) depicts the EDX-dot mapping analysis of x = 0.1, 0.3, and 0.5 Ru-substituted samples. The analysis reveals that Na, Mn, and Ru ions are uniformly distributed on the surface. Additionally, for the x = 0.5 sample, the Ru ions exhibit a rod-type formation, which corresponds to the Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> phase in the sample. This feature can be easily observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a> (Supporting Information). To further probe the structure of the pristine and Ru-substituted cathode materials, HR-TEM and STEM characterizations, displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0002">2</a>, were carried out for the sample where x = 0.4. TEM and STEM images of Ru-substituted cathode materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. HR-TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0002">2c</a>) and SAED pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0002">2d</a>) show that the atomic ordering and hexagonal crystal symmetry after Ru-substitution are similar to that of pristine NMO (P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc symmetry), suggesting that the atomic ordering of the structure along the c-axis is well-preserved after Ru-substitution. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a–d</a> (Supporting Information) exhibits that NMO presents the (002) lattice fringes with a uniform spacing of 5.60&nbsp;Å. In contrast, the spacing of (002) lattice fringes in Ru-substituted NMO is increased to 5.62 Å, suggesting the expansion of Na<sup>+</sup> layer spacing after Ru doping, which is well consistent with the XRD results discussed earlier. Elemental mapping indicates that Na, Mn, Ru, and O elements are homogenously distributed in both pristine NMO and Ru-NMO materials.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2c354f5e-1241-41d3-b6fb-9a4277868064/smll202406332-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2c354f5e-1241-41d3-b6fb-9a4277868064/smll202406332-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/969b485c-f624-4613-84f5-e18fc6544399/smll202406332-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2c354f5e-1241-41d3-b6fb-9a4277868064/smll202406332-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">High magnification a) TEM and b) STEM images of Ru-doped NMO. c) HR-TEM images and SAED pattern of the selected area in (b). e) EDS element mapping of Na, Mn, Ru, and O for the optimized Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ru<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Ru-substituted cathode material.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To gain more insight into the chemical structure of NMO after Ru-doping, time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectroscopy (TOF-SIMS) was employed. TOF-SIMS is well suited for characterizing the surface and buried chemical species. The surface layer of pristine and Ru-substituted NMO are directly visualized in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, respectively. The outer layer of pristine NMO reveals that the surface is mostly comprised of Mn–O and oxygenated species. However, for the Ru-substituted NMO, the Na–O has covered more surface area. For further verification, the normalized depth profiles of the chemical species along with their individual spacial distributions in Ru-doped NMO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0003">3c,d</a>. It could be confirmed from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0003">3d</a> that the outer layer of the surface is comprised of sodium and oxygenated-based species, while the inner layer mainly contains Ru–O and Mn–O. It is worth noting that the Ru-substituted NMO has a high content of RuO<sub>2</sub> (i.e., Ru<sup>4+</sup>) as discussed in the XPS study. In contrast, the MnO<sub>2</sub> (i.e., Mn<sup>4+</sup>) species have lower content at the outer surface, however, it increases with the increase in depth. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0003">3c</a> demonstrates 3D localization of the representative secondary-ion fragments contained in the Ru-doped NMO, whereby the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> can be expected corroborating the XPS and XRD results.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/259d87f6-2e96-46ad-8d3e-7ade9bcf17d9/smll202406332-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/259d87f6-2e96-46ad-8d3e-7ade9bcf17d9/smll202406332-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/28188307-ddb9-4f53-a1fd-fd0247897882/smll202406332-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/259d87f6-2e96-46ad-8d3e-7ade9bcf17d9/smll202406332-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TOF-SIMS chemical mapping after the scavenging process, showing the distribution of NaO<sup>−</sup>, MnO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, and O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> for a) pristine and b) Ru-substituted of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> where x = 0.4. c) Individual spatial distributions and d) TOF-SIMS spectra of Na<sup>−</sup>, O<sup>−</sup>, MnO<sup>−</sup>, MnO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, RuO<sup>−</sup>, and RuO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> secondary-ion fragments over 500&nbsp;s Cs<sup>+</sup> sputtering along the depth profile of the Ru-substituted (x = 0.4) Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> after scavenging processes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further support physicochemical and electrochemical results of the Ru doping in pristine NMO, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. The introduction of Ru modifies the density of states (DOS) near the Fermi level, thereby increasing the availability of electronic states in the conduction band and reducing the bandgap across various sodium intercalation levels (x<sub>Na</sub>). The partial density of states (pDOS) for NMO and Ru-NMO at X<sub>Na</sub> = 0.0, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, and 1.0 are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0004">4a–e</a>. At X<sub>Na</sub> = 0, the bandgap of NMO is ≈1.6&nbsp;eV, which narrows down to 1.147&nbsp;eV upon Ru doping. This reduction in bandgap allows more valence electrons to occupy conduction band orbitals at lower energy levels, consequently enhancing the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0044_R_d233908592e1455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Carbon Energy.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The higher electronic conductivity leads to better rate capability which will be discussed later.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/73db2810-9564-4a47-a822-be163e29c001/smll202406332-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/73db2810-9564-4a47-a822-be163e29c001/smll202406332-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72a3a7af-ce0b-4715-8357-9bd66a84936f/smll202406332-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/73db2810-9564-4a47-a822-be163e29c001/smll202406332-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–e) Partial Density of States (pDOS) of Mn 3d, Ru 4d, and O 2p electrons for NMO and Ru-NMO at different Na content X<sub>Na</sub> = 0, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, and 1. f) Formation energies of NMO (purple) and Ru-NMO (green) with varying Na content.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The oxidation states of transition metals in a compound can be inferred from their magnetization value. This is because the magnetic moment of an atom is directly related to its electronic configuration and number of unpaired electrons, which in turn determines its oxidation state. In pristine NMO, Mn atoms primarily exist in the +3 (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup>) and +4 (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>) oxidation states as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0005">5a</a>. The high-spin state Mn<sup>3+</sup> with its anisotropic electronic structure exhibits Jahn–Teller distortion due to one unpaired electron in the e<sub>g</sub> orbital. In Ru-NMO (Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> where x = 0.4), Ru primarily exists in the 4<sup>+</sup> (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>2</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>) and 5<sup>+</sup> (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>) oxidation states as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0005">5b</a>. As the Ru<sup>4+</sup> and Ru<sup>5+</sup> have no electron in the e<sub>g</sub> orbital, they do not exhibit Jahn–Teller distortion. The higher electronegativity of Ru alters the electronic structure of Mn atoms, causing a redistribution of Mn oxidation states. At 0.75 ≤ X<sub>Na</sub> ≤ 1, some Mn atoms change from 3<sup>+</sup> (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup>) to 2<sup>+</sup> (t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup> e<sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup>). This even distribution of unpaired electrons between the t<sub>2g</sub> and e<sub>g</sub> orbitals further reduces Jahn–Teller distortion. Additionally, the wider distribution of magnetization values for oxygen indicates increased participation in redox reactions due to the higher electronegativity of Ru, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0005">5c</a>. The formation energy curves for both NMO and Ru-NMO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0004">4f</a>. The reduction in formation energy in Ru-NMO is due to the presence of stronger Ru─O bonds and the stabilizing effect of Ru-doping on MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which decreases the Jahn–Teller distortion associated with Mn.<sup>3+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0045_R_d233908592e1578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This leads to a more structurally stable crystal structure, thereby improving cycling stability. Overall the benefit of Ru doping on the structure stability is further evidenced by the magnetization calculations.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4787cae5-8e93-4845-bc4c-d2956a83f986/smll202406332-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4787cae5-8e93-4845-bc4c-d2956a83f986/smll202406332-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e3c7aeb-5bf0-4220-9e70-6098c7231c35/smll202406332-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4787cae5-8e93-4845-bc4c-d2956a83f986/smll202406332-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Magnetization of Mn atom in NMO and Ru-NMO (x = 0.4). b) Magnetization of Ru atom in Ru-NMO. c) Magnetization of O atom in NMO and Ru-NMO. d) Mn─O bond length in NMO at different Na content. e) Mn─O bond length in Ru-NMO at different Na content. f) Ru-O bond length in Ru-NMO at different Na content.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The bond lengths of transition metal layers significantly influence the properties of layered cathode materials. These bond lengths are effected by the difference in the ionic radii as well as oxidation states of the ions present in the crystal structure. In NMO, the Mn─O bond length increases and attains a wider distribution when Na content increases as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0005">5d</a>. A similar trend can be observed in the Mn─O and Ru─O bonds in Ru-NMO structure as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0005">5e,f</a>, respectively. This variation can be attributed to the presence of differently charged transition metals in the crystal structure affecting their bonding with oxygen atoms.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202406332-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202406332-sec-0040-title">2.2 Half-Cells Test Results</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical analysis of the Ru-doped cathode electrodes was performed using a half-cell configuration in CR2032 type coin cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the CV graphs of the cells containing different degree of Ru doping. It is well known that the un-doped P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode has several peaks during the oxidation process. It is speculated that the peaks below 3.8&nbsp;V are due to single-phase intercalation or continuous-phase transition. It has been reported that O2-P2 phase transition takes place above high voltage region in the cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0045_R_d233908592e1630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We believe that the number of oxidation peaks in the CV is explained by the alteration of the crystal field around Na<sup>+</sup> ions which causes the change of the desodiation energy due to phase transitions in the materials. When P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode is substituted, it exhibits only two redox reaction peaks at ≈2.4 and 4.0&nbsp;V (for example, in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes). Previous studies suggest that the phase transitions during charging and discharging of a cell can be prevented by doping or substitution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0020_R_d233908592e1651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochim. Acta.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0021_R_d233908592e1651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When examining the Ru-substituted cathodes, it is evident from the cyclic voltammetry (CV) graphs that two redox peaks appear at 2.25 and 4.04&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202406332-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28a25757-3935-4675-826d-4d442716c30f/smll202406332-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/28a25757-3935-4675-826d-4d442716c30f/smll202406332-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ec21cca1-c4e7-42b0-aa41-4cd93f2fd9d8/smll202406332-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28a25757-3935-4675-826d-4d442716c30f/smll202406332-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202406332-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202406332" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV analysis of the half cells for Ru-substituted cathodes, b) GITT experiment for x = 0.3 and 0.4, c) Diffusion rate-time graphs for x = 0.3 and 0.4, Voltage-capacity graphs for d) x = 0.05, e) 0.1, f) 0.2, g) 0.3, h) 0.4, i) 0.5, and capacity cycle number graphs for j) C/3 and k) 1C rates. X varies in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1-x</sub>Ru<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode structure.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>When we increase the Ru content from 0.05 to 0.2, we observe that the first (≈2.25&nbsp;V) and second redox peaks (4.04&nbsp;V) shifted to lower voltage region and there are two new redox peaks (2.05 and 3.82&nbsp;V) for samples where 0.2≤x≤0.5. As previously discussed in the XRD section, there is a Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub> impurity phase for a high level of Ru doping in the sample, i.e., x = 0.5. When analyzing the differential capacity (dQ/dV) curve of Na<sub>2</sub>RuO<sub>3</sub>, distinct redox reaction peaks are observed ≈2.6&nbsp;V, which do not align with the peaks observed in the Ru-substituted P2-type materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0046_R_d233908592e1695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Electrochem. Commun.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The decrease in the intensity or the emergence of new redox reaction peaks in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) of Ru-substituted materials is caused by alterations in crystal fields. When examining the transition metal (TM) ions within the crystal structure of P2-type materials, the substitution typically involves replacing Ru<sup>4+</sup> with Mn<sup>4+</sup> initially to maintain charge balance in the structure. Subsequently, these ions may migrate to Mn<sup>3+</sup> sites within the lattice. It is known that Ru ions have a higher electronegativity than Mn ions. This difference can lead to an increase in the attractive force for oxygen ions and result in shorter Ru─O bonds compared to Mn─O bonds within the structure. In the second scenario, the Ru<sup>4+</sup> ions exhibit a greater attraction to oxygen ions compared to Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions in the structure, primarily due to the electrostatic force between them. For this assumption, the oxygen ions near the Na<sup>+</sup> layers will move away from these layers. This movement can lead to a reduction in the redox voltage of the cells. The data for the Na-layer distance in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) also aligns with these findings. We hypothesize that there are two distinct structures present in the samples. The shift of the peaks to lower voltage and the appearance of new peaks can be attributed to these structural effects.</p>
            
            <p>The diffusion rate of the Na-ions plays a key role in the battery performance of the cells. There are static and dynamic techniques for the calculation such as Randles–Ševčík equation by measuring scan rate on the peak current (<i>I</i><sub>p</sub>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0047_R_d233908592e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0048_R_d233908592e1728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Electrochem. Sci. Technol.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a–f</a> (Supporting Information) shows the CV graphs of all the samples studied herein at the scan rate of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The I<sub>p</sub>-v<sup>1/2</sup> graphs were presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information) and the calculated diffusion coefficient from the slope of the curves was presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It is found that the static D value was 4.062&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for the sample where x = 0.4 which is the highest value among the others. Similar D values are obtained from the GITT measurement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0049_R_d233908592e1756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the GITT analysis of the x = 0.3 and 0.4 cells. The details of the GITT analysis were given in our previous paper.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0020_R_d233908592e1765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochim. Acta.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The diffusion coefficient can be calculated dynamically at each step by the given equation in reference.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0020_R_d233908592e1770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochim. Acta.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The data is plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6c</a>. It should be noted that the point where the discharge transitions to the charging region in the GITT data exhibits a significant jump in the diffusion rate, which is crucial for the analysis. The first diffusion rate for the OCV was obtained as 1.7&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> and 5.8&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for x = 0.3 and 0.4, respectively. Afterward, they decreased to 1.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−15</sup> and 3.7&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−15</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> at 1.5&nbsp;V during the discharge for x = 0.3 and x = 0.4, respectively. After fully discharging, the D values jumped from 0.95&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup> to 1.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> during the initial portion of the charging process. It is also fair to conclude that when the state of charge increases, the D values decrease in the cell. When we compare the diffusion coefficient values obtained from the GITT (for the first cycle of GITT data) and CV measurements (as 3.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), we find that the results from both methods are consistent with each other. Another important aspect of the CV data is that different scan rates provide insights into the diffusion mechanism of the electrode materials. The peak current (<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) of the CV data can be explained with the power law in the form of <i>i<sub>p</sub></i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i> where <i>v</i> is the scan rate, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the constant parameters. The value of <i>b</i> gives the information for electrochemical reaction mechanisms such as diffusion-controlled (<i>b</i> = ≈0.5) and capacitive reactions (<i>b</i> = ≈1).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0050_R_d233908592e1837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Electrochem. Sci. Technol.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> (Supporting Information) presents the log<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>–log<i>v</i> graphs and the calculated <i>b</i> values are obtained as 0.45, 0.49, 0.75, 0.74, 0.71 for x = 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5, respectively. Increasing the Ru content leads to an increase in the capacitance contribution to the cells for x values between 0.3 and 0.4, but this contribution decreases as the Ru content increases further. It is because of this effect; we observe higher specific capacities than pure NMO cathodes. This observation is explained further below.</p>
            
            <p>The half-cell capacity-voltage graphs of Ru-substituted cathodes for C/3 were presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6d–i</a> and the cycle life analysis of the cells at C/3 were given in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6j</a>. The calculated battery cell performance parameters are given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). In our study, we observed that the highest initial capacity was 176&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for x = 0.3, while the highest capacity retention of 77% was recorded for x = 0.4 cells. We should indicate that the theoretical capacity values of the cells were calculated by the equation of <i>Q = n.F/M</i> (as given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) where <i>Q, n, F, M</i> are capacity, number of Na ions of a unit formula, faraday constant, and <i>M</i> is the molar mass, respectively. When we calculated the capacity of the cathodes, we can see that the cells for x = 0.3–0.5 show higher capacity values than that of the theoretical expectation. To understand this behavior, we evaluated the CV and chemical formula of the un-doped and doped cells. According to the theoretical calculation, the P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode has a capacity of 175&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> which is higher than that of the Ru-substituted one. When examining the unit cell of the cathode, we observe distinct regions rich in manganese (Mn) and ruthenium (Ru), indicating the presence of two structures with identical lattice symmetry. We can infer that the theoretical capacity expectation of the cells should fall between these two phases present in the samples, which is higher than the calculated values. This idea can be supported by the redox peaks in CV graphs which there are extra peaks for 0.2≤x≤0.5 cathodes.</p>
            
            <p>The capacity–voltage graphs of the samples where x = 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 at 1-C rate were presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a–c</a> (Supporting Information) and the cycle life graphs were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202406332-fig-0006">6k</a>. Furthermore, the battery performance parameters were plotted in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The first capacity values at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> rate were obtained as 140, 149.1, and 137.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the samples where x = 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5, respectively. The best capacity retention value, that is as 77% after 100 cycles, was obtained for the cathode where x = 0.4 among the other cells. Though Co is both expensive and toxic, Co substitution can also enhance the cycle life of P-type layered sodium transition metal oxide cathode material. Zhang et. al. synthesized P2/P3–Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Co<sub>0.30</sub>Al<sub>0.06</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode active material via dealloying–annealing method. The cell delivered specific capacity of ≈80 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0051_R_d233908592e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Small.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Li et. al. synthesized Li and Ti doping in to Layered manganese-based oxides producing hexagonal P2-Na<sub>0.643</sub>Li<sub>0.078</sub>Mn<sub>0.827</sub>Ti<sub>0.095</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structure. Their cell also revealed a capacity of 90&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles though it was a half-cell result.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0052_R_d233908592e1931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xu et. al. produced P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes with a capacity of ≈200 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity fade value of 23.7% at 0.1C-rate after 100 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0053_R_d233908592e1947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Ionics.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Marino et. al. produced Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase and they found that the capacity of the cells reached 142 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at C/10 rate after 100 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0054_R_d233908592e1966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - RSC Adv.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Existing studies suggest that substituting Fe in P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has a more favorable impact compared to other transition metals. Since Fe and Ru are in the same column of the periodic table, they may have similar effects despite their different masses. During de-sodiation, the transition metal oxide structure is expected to maintain its symmetry. The mass of ions plays a crucial role in the vibration modes of a crystal structure, affecting factors like amplitude and frequency. Substituting lighter elements with heavier ones can reduce the thermal vibration, potentially improving the stability of the de-sodiated structure's skeleton, as mentioned in the reference.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0055_R_d233908592e1975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we judiciously selected Ru to be an ideal candidate for swapping Mn to enhance the cell performance. The C-rate measurements of the cells were presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) and the highest capacity for C/10-rate was obtained as 155.7 and 164.2 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for x = 0.3 and 0.4, respectively. The capacity of the cells at 3C rate was found as 85.6 and 104.9 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for x = 0.3 and 0.4, respectively. It is therefore concluded that the Ru-substituted P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has good performance at the high rate applications.</p>
            
            <p>The EIS analysis of the samples is given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) with the EQC model presented in the inset of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). There are three different parts in the EQC model; the first is the R<sub>B</sub> which is the equivalent to resistance for the electrolyte, current collectors, and electrode materials, the second region in the EQC which has the first parallel branch (Q<sub>1</sub>-R<sub>1</sub>) can be explained by the SEI layer in the cells and the last part can be explained by the charge-transfer resistance, double layer capacitance and Warburg parameter which are related to diffusion of the Na-ions in the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0056_R_d233908592e2008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Technol.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitting parameters are presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). It is seen that the R<sub>B</sub> values increased by Ru doping and revealed a plateau for the samples where 0.2≤x≤0.4 at ≈5–6&nbsp;Ohm. Although the R<sub>B</sub> value is almost the same for the different SOC values, it can increase by the depletion of the electrolyte and micro crack formation in the particles during the cycling process. It is well known that the first semicircle in EIS measurements can be explained by the impedance of a layer between the electrode and electrolyte which is the second part of the EQC model. We should note that the R<sub>SEI</sub> is very sensitive to the content of the electrolyte in the cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0057_R_d233908592e2023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Electrochim. Acta.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The obtained R<sub>SEI</sub> values in the cells are very close to each other which is the expected since the electrolytes in the cells have the same contents. In the last part of the EQC, R<sub>ct</sub> is related to the kinetics of an electrochemical reaction which is affected by the surface coating, phase transition, bandgap structure, and particle size.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202406332-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0056_R_d233908592e2032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Technol.">56</a>, <a href="#smll202406332-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202406332-bib-0057_R_d233908592e2032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Electrochim.
